Fuxiang</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ma_Gui_(general)" title="Ma Gui (general)">Ma Gui</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ma_Hualong" title="Ma Hualong">Ma Hualong</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ma_Laichi" Dexin</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Wang_Daiyu" title="Wang Daiyu">Wang Daiyu</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Zheng He</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left">Culture</div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><div class="hlist">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_Islamic_cuisine" title="Chinese Islamic cuisine">Cuisine</a> (<a href="/wiki/Xinjiang_cuisine" title="Xinjiang cuisine">Uyghur</a>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Han_Kitab" title="Han Kitab">Han Kitab</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_mosques_in_China" Association of China">Islamic Association of China</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left"><div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li>Cities</li><li>Regions</li></ul></div></div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><div class="hlist">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Hong_Kong" title="Islam in Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a></li>\n<li><a collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left">Groups</div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><div class="hlist">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hui_people" title="Hui people">Hui</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Uyghurs" title="Uyghurs">Uyghurs</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kazakhs_in_China" title="Kazakhs in China">Kazakhs</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dongxiangs" title="Dongxiangs">Dongxiangs</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_in_China" title="Kyrgyz in China">Kyrgyz</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Salar_people" title="Salar people">Salar</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bonans" href="/wiki/Chinese_Tatars" title="Chinese Tatars">Tatars</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Utsul" title="Utsul">Utsul</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Muslims" title="Tibetan Muslims">Tibetans</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border-top:1px solid #21643d;padding-top:0.2em;border-bottom:1px solid #21643d;">\n<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Islam_in_China.jpg"><img alt="Islam in China.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="844" data-file-width="1300" decoding="async" height="10" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Islam_in_China.jpg/16px-Islam_in_China.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Islam_in_China.jpg/24px-Islam_in_China.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Islam_in_China.jpg/32px-Islam_in_China.jpg 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam_in_China" title="Portal:Islam in China">Islam in China portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Islam_in_China" title="Template:Islam in China"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Islam_in_China" title="Template talk:Islam in China"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Islam_in_China&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Zheng He</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">&#37165;&#21644;</span>; 1371&#8211;1433 or 1435) was a Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Chinese_navy" title="History of the Chinese navy">mariner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_exploration" title="Chinese exploration">explorer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_imperial_China" title="Foreign relations of imperial China">diplomat</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fleet_admiral" title="Fleet admiral">fleet admiral</a>, and court eunuch during China\'s <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Ming_dynasty" title="History of the Ming dynasty">early Ming dynasty</a>. He was originally born as <b>Ma He</b> in a Muslim family, later adopted the conferred surname Zheng from Emperor Yongle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-22-3_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-22-3-2">[2]</a></sup> Zheng commanded expeditionary <a href="/wiki/Ming_treasure_voyages" title="Ming treasure voyages">treasure voyages</a> to Southeast Asia, South Asia, Western Asia, and East Africa from 1405 to 1433. His larger ships stretched 120 meters or more in length and carried hundreds of sailors on four tiers of decks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a favorite of the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Jingnan_Campaign" title="Jingnan Campaign">usurpation</a> he assisted, Zheng rose to the top of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Ming_Empire" title="Government of the Ming Empire">imperial hierarchy</a> and served as commander of the southern capital <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> (the capital was later moved to Beijing by the Yongle Emperor). His voyages were long neglected in official Chinese histories but have become well known in China and abroad since the publication of <a href="/wiki/Liang_Qichao" title="Liang Qichao">Liang Qichao</a>\'s <i>Biography of Our Homeland\'s Great Navigator, Zheng He</i> in 1904.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMZX_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-HMZX-5">[5]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Galle_Trilingual_Inscription" title="Galle Trilingual Inscription">trilingual stele</a> left by the navigator was discovered on the island of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a> shortly thereafter.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Early_life_and_family"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Early life and family</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Capture,_castration_and_service"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Capture, castration and career</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Expeditions"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Expeditions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Sailing_charts"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> ships</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Imperial_China"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Imperial China</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Southeast_Asia"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Southeast Asia</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-11"><a href="#Veneration_of_Zheng_He"><span class="tocnumber">8.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Veneration of Zheng He</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-12"><a href="#Malacca"><span class="tocnumber">8.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Malacca</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-13"><a href="#Indonesia"><span class="tocnumber">8.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Indonesia</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Modern_scholarship"><span class="tocnumber">8.3</span> <span class="toctext">Modern scholarship</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Cultural_influence"><span class="tocnumber">8.4</span> <span class="toctext">Cultural influence</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Relics"><span class="tocnumber">8.5</span> <span class="toctext">Relics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Commemoration"><span class="tocnumber">8.6</span> <span class="toctext">Commemoration</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Gallery"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Gallery</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family">Early life and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Zheng He was born Ma He (&#39340;&#21644;)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-11_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-11-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills_1970,_p._5_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mills_1970,_p._5-6">[6]</a></sup> to a <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_China" title="Islam in China">Muslim</a> family<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills_1970,_p._5_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mills_1970,_p._5-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ra87-66_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-ra87-66-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-148_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-148-8">[8]</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Kunyang_Subdistrict" title="Kunyang Subdistrict">Kunyang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunming" title="Kunming">Kunming</a>, Yunnan, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>  He had an older brother and four sisters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-11_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-11-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills_1970,_p._5_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mills_1970,_p._5-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levathes_1996,_62_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levathes_1996,_62-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China_p._621_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-China_p._621-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ma He\'s religious beliefs became <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">all-embracing and eclectic</a> in his adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ra87-66_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-ra87-66-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-148_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-148-8">[8]</a></sup> According to Dreyer (2007), the Liujiagang and Changle inscriptions suggest that Zheng He\'s devotion to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Tianfei</a> (the patron goddess of sailors and seafarers) was the dominant faith to which he adhered, reflecting the goddess\' central role to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_fleet" title="Chinese treasure fleet">treasure fleet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> John Guy mentions, "When Zheng He, the Muslim eunuch leader of the great expeditions to the \'Western Ocean\' (Indian Ocean) in the early fifteenth century, embarked on his voyages, it was from the Divine Woman that he sought protection, as well as at the tombs of the Muslim saints on Lingshan Hill, above the city of Quanzhou."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Zheng He was a great-great-great-grandson of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sayyid_Ajjal_Shams_al-Din_Omar" title="Sayyid Ajjal Shams al-Din Omar">Sayyid Ajjal Shams al-Din Omar</a>, who served in the administration of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> and was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governor_of_Yunnan" title="Governor of Yunnan">governor of Yunnan</a> during the early <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> His great-grandfather was named Bayan and may have been stationed at a Mongol garrison in Yunnan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills_1970,_p._5_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mills_1970,_p._5-6">[6]</a></sup> Zheng He\'s grandfather carried the title <i><a href="/wiki/Hajji" title="Hajji">hajji</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-11_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-11-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-le6-61-2_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-le6-61-2-16">[16]</a></sup> while his father had the <a href="/wiki/Sinicization" title="Sinicization">sinicized</a> surname Ma and also the title <i>hajji</i>, which suggests that they had made the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">pilgrimage to Mecca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-11_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-11-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills_1970,_p._5_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mills_1970,_p._5-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-le6-61-2_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-le6-61-2-16">[16]</a></sup> Peterson (1994) suggests that the <i>Hajji</i> of both his father and grandfather indicated that Zheng He may have had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol-Arab" title="Mongol-Arab">Mongol-Arab</a> ancestry and that he could speak Arabic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the autumn of 1381, a <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming</a> army invaded and <a href="/wiki/Ming_conquest_of_Yunnan" title="Ming conquest of Yunnan">conquered Yunnan</a>, which was then ruled by the Mongol prince <a href="/wiki/Basalawarmi" title="Basalawarmi">Basalawarmi</a>, Prince of Liang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-12_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-12-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1381, Ma Haji (Zheng He\'s father) died in the fighting between the Ming armies and Mongol forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levathes_1996,_62_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levathes_1996,_62-10">[10]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) states that Zheng He\'s father died at age 39 while resisting the Ming conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-12_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-12-18">[18]</a></sup> Levathes (1996) states Zheng He\'s father died at age 37, but it is unclear if he was helping the Mongol army or just caught in the onslaught of battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levathes_1996,_62_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Levathes_1996,_62-10">[10]</a></sup> Wenming, the oldest son, buried their father outside of Kunming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levathes_1996,_62_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Levathes_1996,_62-10">[10]</a></sup> In his capacity as Admiral, Zheng He had an epitaph engraved in honor of his father, composed by the Minister of Rites Li Zhigang on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duanwu_Festival" title="Duanwu Festival">Duanwu Festival</a> of the 3rd year in the Yongle era (1 June 1405).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Capture.2C_castration_and_service"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Capture,_castration_and_service">Capture, castration and service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Capture, castration and service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Ma He was captured by the Ming armies at Yunnan in 1381.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levathes_1996,_62_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-Levathes_1996,_62-10">[10]</a></sup> General <a href="/wiki/Fu_Youde" title="Fu Youde">Fu Youde</a> saw Ma He on a road and approached him in order to inquire about the location of the Mongol pretender.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-58_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-58-20">[20]</a></sup> Ma He responded defiantly by saying that the Mongol pretender had jumped into a lake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-58_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-58-20">[20]</a></sup> Afterwards, the general took him prisoner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-58_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-58-20">[20]</a></sup>  One source states that he was <a href="/wiki/Castration" title="Castration">castrated</a> at the age of 10 and then was placed in the service of the Prince of Yan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> while another source indicates that the castration occurred in 1385.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-58_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-58-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ma He was sent to serve in the household of Zhu Di, the Prince of Yan, who later became the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-12_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-12-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-58_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-58-20">[20]</a></sup> Zhu Di was eleven years older than Ma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-18_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-18-22">[22]</a></sup> While enslaved as a eunuch servant, Ma He eventually gained the confidence of Zhu Di, while Zhu Di as his benefactor would gain the allegiance and loyalty of the young eunuch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Since 1380, the prince had been governing Beiping (later <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-12_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-12-18">[18]</a></sup> which was located near the northern frontier where the hostile Mongol tribes were situated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-58_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-58-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-18_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-18-22">[22]</a></sup> Ma would spend his early life as a soldier on the northern frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-58_20-6"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-58-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-16_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-16-24">[24]</a></sup> He often participated in Zhu Di\'s military campaigns against the Mongols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-18_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-18-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> On 2 March 1390, Ma accompanied the Prince when he commanded <a href="/wiki/Ming_campaign_against_the_Uriankhai" title="Ming campaign against the Uriankhai">his first expedition</a>, which was a great victory as the Mongol commander <a href="/wiki/Naghachu" title="Naghachu">Naghachu</a> surrendered as soon as he realized he had fallen for a deception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG/220px-Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG/330px-Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG/440px-Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kunyang_-_Zheng_He_Park_-_P1350545.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ma Hajji, a Yuan Dynasty official in Yunnan (a descendant of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sayyid_Ajjal_Shams_al-Din_Omar" title="Sayyid Ajjal Shams al-Din Omar">Sayyid Ajjal Shams al-Din Omar</a>), and his young son Ma He, future admiral Zheng He, as imagined by a modern <a href="/wiki/Kunyang_Subdistrict" title="Kunyang Subdistrict">Kunyang</a> sculptor.</div></div></div>\n<p>Eventually, he would gain the confidence and trust of the prince.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-18_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-18-22">[22]</a></sup> Ma was also known as "s&#257;n b&#462;o" during the time of service in the household of the Prince of Yan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-63_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-63-27">[27]</a></sup> This name was a reference to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_Jewels" title="Three Jewels">Three Jewels</a> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#19977;&#23542;</span>, <i>triratna</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> There is also a document saying his name could be <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#19977;&#20445;</span> (<small>lit.</small> "Three Protections", also pronounced <i>s&#257;n b&#462;o</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Ma received a proper education while at Beiping, which he would not have had if he had been placed in the imperial capital Nanjing, as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hongwu" title="Hongwu">Hongwu</a> Emperor did not trust eunuchs and believed that it was better to keep them illiterate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-63_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-63-27">[27]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the Hongwu Emperor purged and exterminated many of the original Ming leadership and gave his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enfeoffment" title="Enfeoffment">enfeoffed</a> sons more military authority, especially those in the north like the Prince of Yan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-19_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-19-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adulthood_and_military_career">Adulthood and military career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Adulthood and military career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Ma He\'s appearance as an adult was recorded: he was seven <i>chi</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[a]</a></sup> tall, had a waist that was five <i>chi</i> in circumference, cheeks and a forehead that was high, a small nose, glaring eyes, teeth that were white and well-shaped as shells, and a voice that was as loud as a bell. It is also recorded that he had great knowledge about warfare and was well-accustomed to battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levathes_1996,_62_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-Levathes_1996,_62-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The young eunuch eventually became a trusted adviser to the prince and assisted him when the <a href="/wiki/Jianwen_Emperor" title="Jianwen Emperor">Jianwen Emperor</a>\'s hostility to his uncle\'s feudal bases prompted the 1399&#8211;1402 <a href="/wiki/Jingnan_Campaign" title="Jingnan Campaign">Jingnan Campaign</a> which ended with the emperor\'s apparent death and the ascension of the Zhu Di, Prince of Yan, as the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a>. In 1393, the Crown Prince had died, thus the deceased prince\'s son became the new heir apparent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-19_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-19-30">[30]</a></sup> By the time the emperor died (24 June 1398), the Prince of Qin and the Prince of Jin had perished, which left Zhu Di, the Prince of Yan, as the eldest surviving son of the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-19_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-19-30">[30]</a></sup> However, Zhu Di\'s nephew succeeded the imperial throne as the <a href="/wiki/Jianwen_Emperor" title="Jianwen Emperor">Jianwen Emperor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-20_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-20-33">[32]</a></sup> In 1398, he issued a policy known as <i>xu&#275;f&#257;n</i>, or "reducing the feudatories", which entails eliminating all the princes by stripping their power and military forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> In August 1399, Zhu Di openly rebelled against his nephew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-21_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-21-35">[34]</a></sup> In 1399, Ma He successfully defended Beiping\'s city reservoir Zhenglunba against the imperial armies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-22-3_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-22-3-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> In January 1402, Zhu Di began with his military campaign to capture the imperial capital Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-70_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-70-37">[36]</a></sup> Zheng He would be one of his commanders during this campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-70_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-70-37">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1402, Zhu Di\'s armies defeated the imperial forces and marched into Nanjing on 13 July 1402.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-70_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-70-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-21-2_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-21-2-38">[37]</a></sup> Zhu Di accepted the elevation to emperor four days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-21-2_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-21-2-38">[37]</a></sup> After ascending the throne as the Yongle Emperor, Zhu Di promoted Ma He as the Grand Director (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#22826;&#30435;</span>, <i>t&#224;i ji&#224;n</i>) of the Directorate of Palace Servants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-21-2_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-21-2-38">[37]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">New Year</a>\'s day on 11 February 1404,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-22-3_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-22-3-2">[2]</a></sup> the Yongle Emperor conferred the surname "Zheng" to Ma He, because he had distinguished himself defending the city reservoir Zhenglunba against imperial forces in the Siege of Beiping of 1399,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-22-3_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-22-3-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-72-3_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-72-3-39">[38]</a></sup> Another reason was that the eunuch commander also distinguished himself during the 1402 campaign to capture the capital Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-72-3_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-72-3-39">[38]</a></sup> It is believed that his choice to confer the surname "Zheng" was because the eunuch\'s horse had been killed during the battle at Zhenglunba near Beiping at the onset of his rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In the new administration, Zheng He served in the highest posts, as Grand Director<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills_1970,_p._5_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-Mills_1970,_p._5-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-China_p._621_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-China_p._621-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> and later as Chief Envoy (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#27491;&#20351;</span>, <i>zh&#232;ngsh&#464;</i>) during his sea voyages. Over the next three decades he conducted seven of these voyages on behalf of the emperor, trading and collecting tribute in the eastern Pacific and Indian Oceans.\n</p><p>In 1424, Admiral Zheng He traveled to <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">Palembang</a> in Sumatra to confer an official seal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[b]</a></sup> and letter of appointment upon Shi Jisun, who was placed in the office of Pacification Commissioner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> The <i>Taizong Shilu</i> 27 February 1424 entry reports that Shi Jisun had sent Qiu Yancheng as envoy to petition the approval of the succession from his father Shi Jinqing, who was the Pacification Commissioner of Palembang, and was given permission from the Yongle Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-95_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-95-44">[42]</a></sup> On 7 September 1424, Zhu Gaozhi had inherited the throne as the Hongxi Emperor after the death of the Yongle Emperor on 12 August 1424.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-1367_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-1367-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> When Zheng He returned from Palembang, he found that the Yongle Emperor had died during his absence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the ascension of Zhu Di\'s son as the <a href="/wiki/Hongxi_Emperor" title="Hongxi Emperor">Hongxi Emperor</a>, the ocean voyages were discontinued and Zheng He was instead appointed as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Defender_of_Nanjing&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Defender of Nanjing (page does not exist)">Defender of Nanjing</a>, the empire\'s southern capital. In that post, he was largely responsible for the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Porcelain_Tower_of_Nanjing" title="Porcelain Tower of Nanjing">Porcelain Tower of Nanjing</a>, an enormous <a href="/wiki/Pagoda" title="Pagoda">pagoda</a> still described as a wonder of the world as late as the 19th century.\n</p><p>On 15 May 1426, the Xuande Emperor ordered the Directorate of Ceremonial to send a letter to Zheng He to reprimand him for a transgression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-1412_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-1412-49">[47]</a></sup> Earlier, an official<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[c]</a></sup> petitioned the emperor to reward workmen who had built temples in Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-1412_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-1412-49">[47]</a></sup> The Xuande Emperor responded negatively to the official for placing the costs to the court instead of the monks themselves, but he realized that Zheng He and his associates had instigated the official.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-1412_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-1412-49">[47]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) noted that the nature of the emperor\'s words indicated that Zheng He\'s behaviour in this situation was the last straw, but that there is too little information about what had transpired beforehand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-1412_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-1412-49">[47]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the Xuande Emperor would eventually come to trust Zheng He.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-1412_49-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-1412-49">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1430, the new <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande Emperor</a> appointed Zheng He to command over a seventh and final expedition into the "Western Ocean" (Indian Ocean).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> In 1431, Zheng He was bestowed with the title "<i>S&#257;nb&#462;o T&#224;i Ji&#224;n</i>" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#19977;&#23542;&#22826;&#30435;</span>), using his informal name Sanbao ("Three Treasures") together with the title of Grand Director.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-7_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-7-52">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>One theory is that Admiral Zheng He died in 1433.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-165_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-165-53">[50]</a></sup> It may have happened during or shortly after the seventh voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-165_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-165-53">[50]</a></sup> Another belief is that Zheng He continued to serve as the defender of Nanjing, dying in 1435.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A tomb was built for Zheng He at the southern slope of Cattle Head Hill, <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lin05_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-lin05-55">[52]</a></sup> The original tomb was a horseshoe-shaped grave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lin05_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-lin05-55">[52]</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Cenotaph" title="Cenotaph">cenotaph</a> believed to contain his clothes and headgear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lin05_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-lin05-55">[52]</a></sup> In 1985, the tomb was rebuilt following a Muslim style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lin05_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-lin05-55">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He adopted the eldest son of his elder brother, who was awarded a hereditary officer rank in the imperial guard.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Expeditions">Expeditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Expeditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ming_treasure_voyages" title="Ming treasure voyages">Ming treasure voyages</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> and expanding Sino-Arab trade during the 14th century had gradually expanded Chinese knowledge of the world: "universal" maps previously only displaying China and its surrounding seas began to expand further and further into the southwest with much more accurate depictions of the extent of Arabia and Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marichang_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-marichang-56">[53]</a></sup> Between 1405 and 1433, the Ming government sponsored seven naval expeditions. The <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> &#8211; disregarding the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang_Ming_Zuxun" title="Huang Ming Zuxun">expressed wishes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuiChunHing_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-HuiChunHing-57">[54]</a></sup> &#8211; designed them to establish a Chinese presence and impose imperial control over the Indian Ocean trade, impress foreign peoples in the Indian Ocean basin, and extend the empire\'s tributary system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It has also been inferred from passages in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ming" title="History of Ming">History of Ming</a> that the initial voyages were launched as part of the emperor\'s attempt to capture his <a href="/wiki/Jianwen_Emperor" title="Jianwen Emperor">escaped predecessor</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marichang_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-marichang-56">[53]</a></sup> which would have made the first voyage the "largest-scale manhunt on water in the history of China".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Zheng He was placed as the admiral in control of the huge fleet and armed forces that undertook these expeditions. <a href="/wiki/Wang_Jinghong" title="Wang Jinghong">Wang Jinghong</a> was appointed his second in command. Preparations were thorough and wide-ranging, including the use of such numerous linguists that a foreign language institute was established at Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marichang_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-marichang-56">[53]</a></sup> Zheng He\'s first voyage departed 11 July 1405, from <a href="/wiki/Suzhou" title="Suzhou">Suzhou</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsai2002_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsai2002-59">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>203</span></sup> and consisted of a fleet of 317<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-60">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tamura_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tamura-62">[59]</a></sup> ships holding almost 28,000 crewmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-60">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ZhengHeShips.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="426" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/ZhengHeShips.gif/220px-ZhengHeShips.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/ZhengHeShips.gif/330px-ZhengHeShips.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/ZhengHeShips.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ZhengHeShips.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 17th-century Chinese <a href="/wiki/Woodcut" title="Woodcut">woodblock</a> print, thought to represent Zheng He\'s ships.</div></div></div>\n<p>Zheng He\'s fleets visited <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, Thailand and <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, India, the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, dispensing and receiving goods along the way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tamura_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tamura-62">[59]</a></sup> Zheng He presented gifts of gold, silver, <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a>, and silk; in return, China received such novelties as ostriches, zebras, camels, and <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsai2002_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tsai2002-59">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>206</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tamura_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tamura-62">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-65">[62]</a></sup> The giraffe he brought back from <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> was considered to be a <i><a href="/wiki/Qilin" title="Qilin">qilin</a></i> and taken as proof of the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">favor of heaven</a> upon the administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-duy-66">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While Zheng He\'s fleet was unprecedented, the routes were not. Zheng He\'s fleet was following long-established, well-mapped routes of trade between China and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a> employed since at least the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>. This fact, along with the use of a more than abundant number of crew members that were regular military personnel, leads some to speculate that these expeditions may have been geared at least partially at spreading China\'s power through expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> Period, the king of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wu_(Ten_Kingdoms)" title="Wu (Ten Kingdoms)">Wu</a> sent a twenty year long diplomatic mission led by Zhu Ying and Kang Tai along the coast of Asia, which reached as far as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup> After centuries of disruption, the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> restored large-scale maritime trade from China in the South Pacific and Indian Oceans, reaching as far as the Arabian peninsula and East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup> When his fleet first arrived in <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a>, there was already a sizable Chinese community. The <i>General Survey of the Ocean Shores</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#28699;&#28079;&#21213;&#35261;</span>, <i>Y&#237;ngy&#225; Sh&#232;ngl&#462;n</i>) composed by the translator <a href="/wiki/Ma_Huan" title="Ma Huan">Ma Huan</a> in 1416 gave very detailed accounts of his observations of people\'s customs and lives in the ports they visited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> He referred to the expatriate Chinese as "<a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a>" people (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#21776;&#20154;</span>, <i>T&#225;ngr&#233;n</i>).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KangnidoMap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/KangnidoMap.jpg/220px-KangnidoMap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/KangnidoMap.jpg/330px-KangnidoMap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/KangnidoMap.jpg/440px-KangnidoMap.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KangnidoMap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kangnido_map" title="Kangnido map">Kangnido map</a> (1402) predates Zheng\'s voyages and suggests that he had quite detailed geographical information on much of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FraMauro1420Ship.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1286" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/FraMauro1420Ship.png/220px-FraMauro1420Ship.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/FraMauro1420Ship.png/330px-FraMauro1420Ship.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/FraMauro1420Ship.png/440px-FraMauro1420Ship.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FraMauro1420Ship.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of the <a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_map" title="Fra Mauro map">Fra Mauro map</a> relating the travels of a <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">junk</a> into the Atlantic Ocean in 1420. The ship also is illustrated above the text.</div></div></div>\n<p>Zheng He generally sought to attain his goals through diplomacy, and his large army awed most would-be enemies into submission. But a contemporary reported that Zheng He "walked like a tiger" and did not shrink from violence when he considered it necessary to impress foreign peoples with China\'s military might.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> He ruthlessly suppressed pirates who had long plagued Chinese and southeast Asian waters. For example, he defeated <a href="/wiki/Chen_Zuyi" title="Chen Zuyi">Chen Zuyi</a>, one of the most feared and respected pirate captains, and returned him to China for execution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup> He also waged a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming-Kotte_War" title="Ming-Kotte War">land war</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kotte" title="Kingdom of Kotte">Kingdom of Kotte</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a>, and he made displays of military force when local officials threatened his fleet in Arabia and East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> From his fourth voyage, he brought envoys from thirty states who traveled to China and paid their respects at the Ming court.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In 1424, the Yongle Emperor died. His successor, the <a href="/wiki/Hongxi_Emperor" title="Hongxi Emperor">Hongxi Emperor</a> (r. 1424&#8211;1425), stopped the voyages during his short reign. Zheng He made one more voyage during the reign of Hongxi\'s son, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor_of_China" title="Xuande Emperor of China">Xuande Emperor</a> (r. 1426&#8211;1435) but, after that, the voyages of the Chinese treasure ship fleets were ended. Xuande believed his father\'s decision to halt the voyages had been meritorious and thus "there would be no need to make a detailed description of his grandfather\'s sending Zheng He to the Western Ocean."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuiChunHing_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-HuiChunHing-57">[54]</a></sup> The voyages "were contrary to the rules stipulated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang_Ming_Zuxun" title="Huang Ming Zuxun">Huang Ming Zuxun</a>" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#30343;&#26126;&#31062;&#35347;</span>), the dynastic foundation documents laid down by the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuiChunHing_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-HuiChunHing-57">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Some far-off countries pay their tribute to me at much expense and through great difficulties, all of which are by no means my own wish. Messages should be forwarded to them to reduce their tribute so as to avoid high and unnecessary expenses on both sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>They further violated longstanding Confucian principles. They were only made possible by (and therefore continued to represent) a triumph of the Ming\'s eunuch faction over the administration\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrat" title="Scholar-bureaucrat">scholar-bureaucrats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marichang_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-marichang-56">[53]</a></sup> Upon Zheng He\'s death and his faction\'s fall from power, his successors sought to minimize him in official accounts, along with continuing attempts to destroy all records related to the Jianwen Emperor or the manhunt to find him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuiChunHing_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-HuiChunHing-57">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although unmentioned in the official dynastic histories, Zheng He probably died during the treasure fleet\'s last voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marichang_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-marichang-56">[53]</a></sup> Although he has a tomb in China, it is empty: he was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buried_at_sea" title="Buried at sea">buried at sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Zheng He led seven expeditions to the "Western" or Indian Ocean. Zheng He brought back to China many trophies and envoys from more than thirty kingdoms &#8211; including King Vira Alakeshwara of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a>, who came to China as a captive to apologize to the Emperor for offenses against his mission.\n</p><p>Zheng himself wrote of his travels:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>We have traversed more than 100,000 <i><a href="/wiki/Li_(unit)" title="Li (unit)">li</a></i> of immense water spaces and have beheld in the ocean huge waves like mountains rising in the sky, and we have set eyes on barbarian regions far away hidden in a blue transparency of light vapors, while our sails, loftily unfurled like clouds day and night, continued their course [as rapidly] as a star, traversing those savage waves as if we were treading a public thoroughfare...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sailing_charts">Sailing charts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Sailing charts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_geography" title="Chinese geography">Chinese geography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mao_Kun_map" title="Mao Kun map">Mao Kun map</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WuBeiZhi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="562" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/WuBeiZhi.jpg/220px-WuBeiZhi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/WuBeiZhi.jpg/330px-WuBeiZhi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/WuBeiZhi.jpg/440px-WuBeiZhi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WuBeiZhi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of a set of maps of Zheng He\'s missions (&#37073;&#21644;&#33322;&#28023;&#22270;), also known as the <a href="/wiki/Mao_Kun_map" title="Mao Kun map">Mao Kun map</a>, 1628</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1036" data-file-width="1454" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/220px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/330px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/440px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A section of the <i>Wubei Zhi</i> oriented east: India in the upper left, Sri Lanka upper right, and Africa along the bottom.</div></div></div>\n<p>Zheng He\'s sailing charts, the <a href="/wiki/Mao_Kun_map" title="Mao Kun map">Mao Kun map</a>, were published in a book entitled the <i><a href="/wiki/Wubei_Zhi" title="Wubei Zhi">Wubei Zhi</a></i> (<i>A Treatise on Armament Technology</i>) written in 1621 and published in 1628 but traced back to Zheng He\'s and earlier voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> It was originally a strip map 20.5&#160;cm by 560&#160;cm that could be rolled up, but was divided into 40 pages which vary in scale from 7 miles/inch in the Nanjing area to 215 miles/inch in parts of the African coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Investigation into folios 19V to 20R of the Mao Kun Map which covers the Indian Ocean including South India, Sri Lanka, the Maldives, and East Africa suggests that it is a composite of four maps, one for Sri Lanka, one for South India one for the Maldives and one for around 400&#160;km of the East African coast, no further south than 6 degrees south of the Equator. Each of these maps is positioned at a different orientation to fit with the ocean currents and winds required of a sailing chart, rather than a formal map. The analysis also suggests that Arabic-speaking pilots with a detailed knowledge of the African coast were involved in the cartography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is little attempt to provide an accurate 2-D representation; instead the sailing instructions are given using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_directions" title="Chinese directions">24-point compass system</a> with a Chinese symbol for each point, together with a sailing time or distance, which takes account of the local currents and winds. Sometimes depth soundings are also provided. It also shows bays, estuaries, capes and islands, ports and mountains along the coast, important landmarks such as pagodas and temples, and shoal rocks. Of 300 named places outside China, more than 80% can be confidently located. There are also fifty observations of stellar altitude.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_of_the_ships">Size of the ships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Size of the ships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Traditional and popular accounts of Zheng He\'s voyages have described a great fleet of gigantic ships, far larger than any other wooden ships in history.\n</p><p>Chinese records<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup> state that Zheng He\'s fleet sailed as far as East Africa. According to medieval Chinese sources, Zheng He commanded seven expeditions. The 1405 expedition consisted of 27,800 men and a fleet of 62 treasure ships supported by approximately 190 smaller ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> The fleet included:\n</p>\n<ul><li>"<a href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_ship" title="Chinese treasure ship">Chinese treasure ships</a>" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#23453;&#33337;</span>, <i>B&#462;o Chu&#225;n</i>), used by the commander of the fleet and his deputies (nine-masted, about 127 metres (417 feet) long and 52 metres (171 feet) wide), according to later writers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>\n<li>Equine ships (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#39340;&#33337;</span>, <i>M&#462; Chu&#225;n</i>), carrying horses and tribute goods and repair material for the fleet (eight-masted, about 103&#160;m (338&#160;ft) long and 42&#160;m (138&#160;ft) wide).</li>\n<li>Supply ships (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#31918;&#33337;</span>, <i>Li&#225;ng Chu&#225;n</i>), containing staple for the crew (seven-masted, about 78&#160;m (256&#160;ft) long and 35&#160;m (115&#160;ft) wide).</li>\n<li>Troop transports (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#20853;&#33337;</span>, <i>B&#299;ng Chu&#225;n</i>), six-masted, about 67&#160;m (220&#160;ft) long and 25&#160;m (82&#160;ft) wide.</li>\n<li>Fuchuan warships (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#31119;&#33337;</span>, <i>F&#250; Chu&#225;n</i>), five-masted, about 50&#160;m (160&#160;ft) long.</li>\n<li>Patrol boats (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#22352;&#33337;</span>, <i>Zu&#242; Chu&#225;n</i>), eight-oared, about 37&#160;m (121&#160;ft) long.</li>\n<li>Water tankers (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#27700;&#33337;</span>, <i>Shu&#464; Chu&#225;n</i>), with 1 month\'s supply of fresh water.</li></ul>\n<p>Six more expeditions took place, from 1407 to 1433, with fleets of comparable size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>If the accounts can be taken as factual Zheng He\'s treasure ships were mammoth ships with nine masts, four decks, and were capable of accommodating more than 500 passengers, as well as a massive amount of cargo. <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a> both described multi-masted ships carrying 500 to 1,000 passengers in their translated accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Da_Conti" title="Niccol&#242; Da Conti">Niccol&#242; Da Conti</a>, a contemporary of Zheng He, was also an eyewitness of ships in Southeast Asia, claiming to have seen 5 masted junks weighing about 2,000 tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup> There are even some sources that claim some of the treasure ships might have been as long as 600 feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> On the ships were navigators, explorers, sailors, doctors, workers, and soldiers along with the translator and diarist <a href="/wiki/Gong_Zhen" title="Gong Zhen">Gong Zhen</a>.\n</p><p>The largest ships in the fleet, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_ships" title="Chinese treasure ships">Chinese treasure ships</a> described in Chinese chronicles, would have been several times <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_world%27s_largest_wooden_ships" title="List of world\'s largest wooden ships">larger than any other wooden ship</a> ever recorded in history, surpassing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_ship_L%27Orient_(1791)" title="French ship L\'Orient (1791)"><i>l\'Orient</i></a>, 65 metres (213.3&#160;ft) long, which was built in the late 18th century. The first ships to attain 126&#160;m (413.4&#160;ft) long were 19th century steamers with iron hulls. Some scholars argue that it is highly unlikely that Zheng He\'s ship was 450 feet (137.2&#160;m) in length, some estimating that they were 390&#8211;408 feet (118.9&#8211;124.4&#160;m) long and 160&#8211;166 feet (48.8&#8211;50.6&#160;m) wide instead<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup> while others put them as small as 200&#8211;250 feet (61.0&#8211;76.2&#160;m) in length, which would make them smaller than the equine, supply, and troop ships in the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One explanation for the seemingly inefficient size of these colossal ships was that the largest 44 Zhang treasure ships were merely used by the Emperor and imperial bureaucrats to travel along the Yangtze for court business, including reviewing Zheng He\'s expedition fleet. The Yangtze river, with its calmer waters, may have been navigable by these treasure ships. Zheng He, a court eunuch, would not have had the privilege in rank to command the largest of these ships, seaworthy or not. The main ships of Zheng He\'s fleet were instead 6 masted 2000-liao ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical1_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Technical1-90">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup> This would give <a href="/wiki/Builder%27s_Old_Measurement" title="Builder\'s Old Measurement">burthen</a> of 500 tons and a displacement tonnage of about 800 tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Technical1_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Technical1-90">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1971481_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1971481-92">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperial_China">Imperial China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Imperial China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chen_Zhang%27s_painting_of_a_giraffe_and_its_attendant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="446" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Chen_Zhang%27s_painting_of_a_giraffe_and_its_attendant.jpg/170px-Chen_Zhang%27s_painting_of_a_giraffe_and_its_attendant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8f/Chen_Zhang%27s_painting_of_a_giraffe_and_its_attendant.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chen_Zhang%27s_painting_of_a_giraffe_and_its_attendant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The pet giraffe of the <a href="/wiki/Ghiyasuddin_Azam_Shah" title="Ghiyasuddin Azam Shah">Sultan of Bengal</a>, brought from the Somali <a href="/wiki/Ajuran_Sultanate" title="Ajuran Sultanate">Ajuran Empire</a>, and later taken to China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup> in the thirteenth year of Yongle (1415).</div></div></div>\n<p>In the decades after the last voyage, Imperial officials minimized the importance of Zheng He and his expeditions throughout the many regnal and dynastic histories they compiled. The information in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Taizong_Shilu" title="Ming Taizong Shilu">Yongle</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Xuanzong_Shilu" title="Ming Xuanzong Shilu">Xuande Emperors\' official annals</a> was incomplete and even erroneous; other official publications omitted them completely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMZX_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-HMZX-5">[5]</a></sup> Although some have seen this as a conspiracy seeking to eliminate memories of the voyages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heyhei_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-heyhei-97">[94]</a></sup> it is likely that the records were dispersed throughout several departments and the expeditions &#8211; unauthorized by (and in fact, counter to) the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang_Ming_Zu_Xun" title="Huang Ming Zu Xun">injunctions of the dynastic founder</a> &#8211; presented a kind of embarrassment to the dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMZX_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-HMZX-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>State-sponsored Ming naval efforts declined dramatically after Zheng\'s voyages. Starting in the early 15th century, China experienced increasing pressure from the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Yuan_dynasty" title="Northern Yuan dynasty">surviving Yuan Mongols</a> from the north. The relocation of the capital north to Beijing exacerbated this threat dramatically. At considerable expense, China launched annual military expeditions from Beijing to weaken the Mongolians. The expenditures necessary for these land campaigns directly competed with the funds necessary to continue naval expeditions. Further, in 1449, Mongolian cavalry ambushed a land expedition personally led by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhengtong_Emperor" title="Zhengtong Emperor">Zhengtong Emperor</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tumu_Fortress" title="Battle of Tumu Fortress">Tumu Fortress</a>, less than a day\'s march from the walls of the capital. The Mongolians wiped out the Chinese army and captured the emperor. This battle had two salient effects. First, it demonstrated the clear threat posed by the northern nomads. Second, the Mongols caused a political crisis in China when they released the emperor after his half-brother had already ascended and declared the new <a href="/wiki/Jingtai_Emperor" title="Jingtai Emperor">Jingtai era</a>. Not until 1457 and the restoration of the former emperor did political stability return. Upon his return to power, China abandoned the strategy of annual land expeditions and instead embarked upon a massive and expensive expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall of China</a>. In this environment, funding for naval expeditions simply did not happen.\n</p><p>However, missions from Southeast Asia continued to arrive for decades. Depending on local conditions, they could reach such frequency that the court found it necessary to restrict them: the <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Ming" title="History of Ming">History of Ming</a></i> records imperial edicts forbidding Java, Champa, and Siam from sending their envoys more often than once every three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-98">[95]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2362" data-file-width="3543" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg/220px-Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg/330px-Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg/440px-Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mi%E1%BA%BFu_Nh%E1%BB%8B_Ph%E1%BB%A7.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The &#212;ng B&#7893;n Temple, built to venerate Zheng He, or B&#7893;n &#272;&#7847;u C&#244;ng in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saigon" title="Saigon">Saigon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a></div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Veneration_of_Zheng_He">Veneration of Zheng He</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Veneration of Zheng He">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cakra_Donya.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1248" data-file-width="1664" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Cakra_Donya.JPG/220px-Cakra_Donya.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Cakra_Donya.JPG/330px-Cakra_Donya.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Cakra_Donya.JPG/440px-Cakra_Donya.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cakra_Donya.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cakra Donya Bell, a gift from Zheng He to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pasai" title="Pasai">Pasai</a>, now located at the Museum Aceh in <a href="/wiki/Banda_Aceh" title="Banda Aceh">Banda Aceh</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_diaspora" title="Chinese diaspora">Chinese diaspora</a> in Southeast Asia, Zheng He became a figure of folk veneration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup> Even some of his crew members who happened to stay in this or that port sometimes did as well, such as "Poontaokong" on Sulu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-98">[95]</a></sup> The temples of this cult &#8211; called after either of his names, Cheng Hoon or Sam Po &#8211; are peculiar to overseas Chinese except for a single temple in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hongjian&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hongjian (page does not exist)">Hongjian</a> originally constructed by a returned Filipino Chinese in the Ming dynasty and rebuilt by another Filipino Chinese after the original was destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-98">[95]</a></sup> (The same village of Hongjian, in <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Jiaomei" title="Jiaomei">Jiaomei</a> township, is also the ancestral home of <a href="/wiki/Corazon_Aquino" title="Corazon Aquino">Corazon Aquino</a>.)\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malacca">Malacca</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Malacca">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG/220px-%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG/330px-%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG/440px-%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E9%A9%AC%E5%85%AD%E7%94%B2%E4%B8%89%E5%AE%9D%E5%BA%99.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Poh_San_Teng_Temple" title="Poh San Teng Temple">Sam Po Kong Temple</a> in Malacca.</div></div></div>\n<p>The oldest and most important Chinese temple in Malacca is the 17th-century Cheng Hoon Teng, dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Guanyin" title="Guanyin">Guanyin</a>. During <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">Dutch colonial rule</a>, the head of the Cheng Hoon Temple was appointed chief over the community\'s Chinese inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_98-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-98">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following Zheng He\'s arrival, the sultan and sultana of Malacca visited China at the head of over 540 of their subjects, bearing ample tribute. Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mansur_Shah_of_Malacca" title="Mansur Shah of Malacca">Mansur Shah</a> (r. 1459&#8211;1477) later dispatched Tun Perpatih Putih as his envoy to China, carrying a letter from the sultan to the Ming emperor. The letter requested the hand of an imperial daughter in marriage. Malay (but not Chinese) annals record that, in the year 1459, a princess named <a href="/wiki/Hang_Li_Po" title="Hang Li Po">Hang Li Po</a> or Hang Liu was sent from China to marry the sultan. The princess came with 500 high-ranking young men and a few hundred handmaidens as her entourage. They eventually settled in <a href="/wiki/Bukit_Cina" title="Bukit Cina">Bukit Cina</a>. It is believed that a significant number of them married into the local populace, creating the descendants now known as the <a href="/wiki/Peranakan" title="Peranakan">Peranakan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malacca_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-malacca-100">[97]</a></sup> Owing to this supposed lineage, the Peranakan still use special honorifics: <i>Baba</i> for the men and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nyonya" title="Nyonya">Nyonya</a></i> for the women.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">Indonesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Indonesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stamps_of_Indonesia,_026-05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="306" data-file-width="499" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg/220px-Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg/330px-Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg/440px-Stamps_of_Indonesia%2C_026-05.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stamps_of_Indonesia,_026-05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stamp from <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> commemorating Zheng He\'s voyages to secure the maritime routes, usher urbanization and assist in creating a common prosperity throughout continents and cultures.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cheng_Hoo1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="923" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Cheng_Hoo1.JPG/220px-Cheng_Hoo1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Cheng_Hoo1.JPG/330px-Cheng_Hoo1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Cheng_Hoo1.JPG/440px-Cheng_Hoo1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cheng_Hoo1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Zheng Hoo Mosque in <a href="/wiki/Surabaya" title="Surabaya">Surabaya</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Indonesian Chinese have established temples to Zheng He in <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cirebon" title="Cirebon">Cirebon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surabaya" title="Surabaya">Surabaya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Semarang" title="Semarang">Semarang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_98-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-98">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1961, the <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Indonesia" title="Ethnic groups in Indonesia">Indonesian</a> Islamic leader and scholar <a href="/wiki/Hamka" title="Hamka">Hamka</a> credited Zheng He with an important role in the development of Islam in Indonesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup> The <i>Brunei Times</i> credits Zheng He with building Chinese Muslim communities in <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">Palembang</a> and along the shores of <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>. These Muslims allegedly followed the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> school in the Chinese language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup> This Chinese Muslim community was led by Hajji <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Yan_Ying_Yu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Yan Ying Yu (page does not exist)">Yan Ying Yu</a>, who urged his followers to assimilate and take local names. The Chinese trader <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sun_Long_(merchant)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sun Long (merchant) (page does not exist)">Sun Long</a> even supposedly adopted the son of the king of <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">Majapahit</a> and his Chinese wife, a son who went on to become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Demak#Raden_Patah" title="Sultanate of Demak">Raden Patah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup> Amid this assimilation (and loss of contact with China itself), the Hanafi Islam became absorbed by the local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" title="Shafi\'i">Shafi\'i</a> school and the presence of distinctly ethnic Chinese Muslims dwindled to almost nothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup> The <i>Malay Annals</i> also record a number of Hanafi mosques &#8211;
