in <a href="/wiki/Semarang" title="Semarang">Semarang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancol" title="Ancol">Ancol</a>, for instance &#8211; were converted directly into temples of the Zheng He cult during the 1460s and \'70s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tata_98-5"><a href="#cite_note-Tata-98">[95]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sam_Poo_Kong" title="Sam Poo Kong">Sam Poo Kong</a> temple in Semarang was built to commemorate Zheng He\'s voyage to Java.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_scholarship">Modern scholarship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Modern scholarship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the 1950s, historians such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Fairbank" title="John Fairbank">John Fairbank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Needham" title="Joseph Needham">Joseph Needham</a> popularized the idea that after Zheng He\'s voyages China turned away from the seas due to the <i><a href="/wiki/Haijin" title="Haijin">Haijin</a></i> edict and was isolated from European technological advancements. Modern historians point out that Chinese maritime commerce did not totally stop after Zheng He, that Chinese ships continued to participate in Southeast Asian commerce until the 19th century, and that active Chinese trading with India and East Africa continued long after the time of Zheng. Moreover, <a href="/wiki/Historical_revisionism" title="Historical revisionism">revisionist historians</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Jack_Goldstone" title="Jack Goldstone">Jack Goldstone</a> argue that the Zheng He voyages ended for practical reasons that did not reflect the technological level of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup> Although the Ming dynasty did ban shipping with the <i>Haijin</i> edict, this was a policy of the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a> that long preceded Zheng He and the ban &#8211; so obviously disregarded by the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> &#8211; was eventually lifted entirely. However, the ban on maritime shipping did force countless numbers of people into smuggling and <a href="/wiki/Wokou" title="Wokou">piracy</a>. Neglect of the imperial navy and Nanjing dockyards after Zheng He\'s voyages left the coast highly vulnerable to Japanese <a href="/wiki/Wokou" title="Wokou">Wokou</a> during the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang2013_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang2013-106">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grygiel2006_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grygiel2006-107">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_von_Glahn&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Richard von Glahn (page does not exist)">Richard von Glahn</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UCLA" title="UCLA">UCLA</a> professor of Chinese history, commented that most treatments of Zheng He present him wrongly: they "offer counterfactual arguments" and "emphasize China\'s missed opportunity." This "narrative emphasizes the failure" instead of the accomplishments, despite his assertion that "Zheng He reshaped Asia." Glahn argues that maritime history in the 15th century was essentially the Zheng He story and the effects of his voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glaan_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-glaan-108">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_influence">Cultural influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cultural influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Despite the official neglect, the adventures of the fleet captured the imagination of some Chinese and novelizations of the voyages occurred, such as the <i>Romance of the Three-Jeweled Eunuch</i> in 1597.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heyhei_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-heyhei-97">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On his travels, Zheng He built mosques<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> while also spreading the worship of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Mazu</a>. He apparently never found time for a <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">pilgrimage</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> but did send sailors there on his last voyage. He played an important part in developing relations between China and Islamic countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup> Zheng He also visited Muslim shrines of Islamic holy men in the <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In modern times, interest in Zheng He revived substantially. In <a href="/wiki/Vernor_Vinge" title="Vernor Vinge">Vernor Vinge</a>\'s 1999 science-fiction novel <i><a href="/wiki/A_Deepness_in_the_Sky" title="A Deepness in the Sky">A Deepness in the Sky</a></i>, an interstellar society of commercial traders in human space are named the Qeng Ho after the admiral. The expeditions featured prominently in <a href="/wiki/Heather_Terrell" title="Heather Terrell">Heather Terrell</a>\'s 2005 novel <i>The Map Thief</i>. For the 600th anniversary of Zheng He\'s voyages in 2005, the China\'s <a href="/wiki/China_Central_Television" title="China Central Television">CCTV</a> produced a special television series <i><a href="/wiki/Zheng_He_Xia_Xiyang_(TV_series)" title="Zheng He Xia Xiyang (TV series)">Zheng He Xia Xiyang</a></i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Gallen_Lo" title="Gallen Lo">Gallen Lo</a> as Zheng He. He is also mentioned in part of the main storyline of the first-person shooter game <a href="/wiki/Far_Cry_3" title="Far Cry 3">Far Cry 3</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relics">Relics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Relics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<dl><dt>Nanjing Temple of Mazu</dt></dl>\n<p>Zheng He built the Tianfei Palace (&#22825;&#22915;&#23467;, <i>Ti&#257;nf&#275;ig&#333;ng</i>, <small>lit.</small> "Palace of the Celestial Wife"), a temple in honor of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mythology" title="Chinese mythology">goddess</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Mazu</a>, in Nanjing after the fleet returned from its first western voyage in 1407.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Taicang Stele</dt></dl>\n<p>The "Deed of Foreign Connection and Exchange" (&#36890;&#30058;&#20107;&#36321;) or "Tongfan Deed Stele" is located in the Tianfei Palace in <a href="/wiki/Liuhe,_Taicang" title="Liuhe, Taicang">Liuhe, Taicang</a>, whence the expeditions first departed. The <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> was submerged and lost, but has been rebuilt.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Nanshan Stele</dt></dl>\n<p>In order to thank the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Celestial Wife</a> for her blessings, Zheng He and his colleagues rebuilt the Tianfei Palace in Nanshan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Changle" title="Changle">Changle</a> county, in Fujian province as well prior to departing on their last voyage. At the renovated temple, they raised a stele entitled "A Record of Tianfei Showing Her Presence and Power" (&#22825;&#22915;&#38728;&#25033;&#20043;&#35352;, <i> Ti&#257;nf&#275;i L&#237;ng Y&#299;ng zh&#299; J&#236;</i>), discussing their earlier voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Sri Lankan Stele</dt></dl>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Galle_Trilingual_Inscription" title="Galle Trilingual Inscription">Galle Trilingual Inscription</a> in Sri Lanka was discovered in the city of <a href="/wiki/Galle" title="Galle">Galle</a> in 1911 and is preserved at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Colombo" title="National Museum of Colombo">National Museum of Colombo</a>. The three languages used in the inscription were Chinese, <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">Tamil</a> and Persian. The inscription praises <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> and describes the fleet\'s donations to the famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tondeswaram_temple" title="Tondeswaram temple">Tenavarai Nayanar temple</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tondeswaram" title="Tondeswaram">Tondeswaram</a> frequented by both <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Tomb and Museum</dt></dl>\n<p>Zheng He\'s tomb in Nanjing has been repaired and a small museum built next to it, although his body was buried at sea off the <a href="/wiki/Malabar_Coast" title="Malabar Coast">Malabar Coast</a> near Calicut in western India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup> However, his sword and other personal possessions were interred in a Muslim tomb inscribed in Arabic.\n</p><p>The tomb of Zheng He\'s assistant <a href="/wiki/Hong_Bao" title="Hong Bao">Hong Bao</a> was recently unearthed in Nanjing, as well.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Commemoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the People\'s Republic of China, 11 July is <a href="/wiki/China_National_Maritime_Day" title="China National Maritime Day">Maritime Day</a> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#20013;&#22269;&#33322;&#28023;&#26085;</span>, <i>Zh&#333;nggu&#243; H&#225;ngh&#462;i R&#236;</i>) and is devoted to the memory of Zheng He\'s first voyage. Initially <a href="/wiki/Kunming_Changshui_International_Airport" title="Kunming Changshui International Airport">Kunming Changshui International Airport</a> was to be named Zheng He International Airport.\n</p><p>In 2015 <a href="/wiki/Emotion_Media_Factory" title="Emotion Media Factory">Emotion Media Factory</a> dedicated a special multimedia show "Zheng He is coming" for amusement park <a href="/wiki/Romon_U-Park" title="Romon U-Park">Romon U-Park</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ningbo" title="Ningbo">Ningbo</a>, China). The show became a finalist of the amusement industry prestigious Brass Rings Awards by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAAPA" title="IAAPA">IAAPA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery">Gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A <i>chi</i> is thought to vary between 10.5 to 12 inches (Dreyer 2007, 19).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>Taizong Shilu</i> 27 February 1424 entry reports that Zheng He was sent to deliver the seal, because the old seal was destroyed in a fire. The <i>Xuanzong Shilu</i> 17 September 1425 entry reports that Zhang Funama delivered a seal, because the old seal was destroyed in a fire. The later <i>Mingshi</i> compilers seem to have combined these accounts, remarking that Shi Jisun\'s succession was approved in 1424 and that a new seal was delivered in 1425, suggesting that only one seal was destroyed by fire. (Dreyer 2007, 96)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Unnamed official who served as a Department Director under the Ministry of Works, who earlier had departed for Nanjing to supervise the renovation of government buildings and to reward the skilled workers (Dreyer 2007, 141).</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zheng_He&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-dr7-11-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-dr7-11_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dr7-11_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dr7-11_1-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dr7-11_1-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dr7-11_1-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dr7-11_1-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Dreyer 2007, 11.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-dr7-22-3-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-dr7-22-3_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dr7-22-3_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dr7-22-3_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dr7-22-3_2-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Dreyer 2007, 22&#8211;23.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Pollard, Elizabeth (2015). <i>Worlds Together Worlds Apart</i>.
